<h1 class="firstHeading" id="firstHeading" lang="en"><i>Hayabusa</i></h1> <div class="mw-body-content" id="bodyContent">
<div class="noprint" id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div> <div id="contentSub"><span class="mw-redirectedfrom">  (Redirected from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/w/index.php?title=Hayabusa_(spacecraft)&amp;redirect=no" title="Hayabusa (spacecraft)">Hayabusa (spacecraft)</a>)</span></div>
<div id="jump-to-nav"></div> <a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>
<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#p-search">Jump to search</a>

<p><i><b>Hayabusa</b></i><span style="font-weight: normal"> (<a href="/wiki/Japanese_language" title="Japanese language">Japanese</a>: <span lang="ja">はやぶさ</span>, "<a href="/wiki/Peregrine_falcon" title="Peregrine falcon">Peregrine falcon</a>")</span> was a <a href="/wiki/Robotic_spacecraft" title="Robotic spacecraft">robotic spacecraft</a> developed by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Japan_Aerospace_Exploration_Agency" title="Japan Aerospace Exploration Agency">Japan Aerospace Exploration Agency</a> (JAXA) to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sample_return_mission" title="Sample return mission">return a sample of material</a> from a small <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Near-Earth_asteroid" title="Near-Earth asteroid">near-Earth asteroid</a> named <a href="/wiki/25143_Itokawa" title="25143 Itokawa">25143 Itokawa</a> to <a href="/wiki/Earth" title="Earth">Earth</a> for further analysis.
<i>Hayabusa</i>, formerly known as <b>MUSES-C</b> for <b>Mu Space Engineering Spacecraft C</b>, was launched on 9 May 2003 and rendezvoused with Itokawa in mid-September 2005.  After arriving at Itokawa, <i>Hayabusa</i> studied the asteroid's shape, spin, topography, colour, composition, density, and history. In November 2005, it landed on the asteroid and collected samples in the form of tiny grains of asteroidal material, which were returned to Earth aboard the spacecraft on 13 June 2010.
</p><p>The spacecraft also carried a detachable minilander, <i><a href="#MINERVA_mini-lander">MINERVA</a></i>, which failed to reach the surface.
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Mission_firsts">Mission firsts</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hayabusa&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Mission firsts">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:D._Moura_and_J._Kawaguchi_IAC_2010.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1683" data-file-width="2360" decoding="async" height="157" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d8/D._Moura_and_J._Kawaguchi_IAC_2010.jpg/220px-D._Moura_and_J._Kawaguchi_IAC_2010.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d8/D._Moura_and_J._Kawaguchi_IAC_2010.jpg/330px-D._Moura_and_J._Kawaguchi_IAC_2010.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d8/D._Moura_and_J._Kawaguchi_IAC_2010.jpg/440px-D._Moura_and_J._Kawaguchi_IAC_2010.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:D._Moura_and_J._Kawaguchi_IAC_2010.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Denis J. P. Moura (left) and Junichiro Kawaguchi (right) at the 2010 <a href="/wiki/International_Astronautical_Congress" title="International Astronautical Congress">International Astronautical Congress</a> (IAC)</div></div></div>
<p>Other spacecraft, notably <i><a href="/wiki/Galileo_(spacecraft)" title="Galileo (spacecraft)">Galileo</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/NEAR_Shoemaker" title="NEAR Shoemaker">NEAR Shoemaker</a></i> (both launched by <a href="/wiki/NASA" title="NASA">NASA</a>), had visited asteroids before, but the <i>Hayabusa</i> mission was the first attempt to return an asteroid sample to <a href="/wiki/Earth" title="Earth">Earth</a> for analysis.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">[1]</a></sup>
</p><p>In addition, <i>Hayabusa</i> was the first spacecraft designed to deliberately land on an asteroid and then take off again (<i>NEAR Shoemaker</i> made a controlled descent to the surface of <a href="/wiki/433_Eros" title="433 Eros">433 Eros</a> in 2000, but it was not designed as a lander and was eventually deactivated after it arrived). Technically, <i>Hayabusa</i> was not designed to "land"; it simply touches the surface with its sample capturing device and then moves away. However, it was the first craft designed from the outset to make physical contact with the surface of an asteroid. Junichiro Kawaguchi of the <a href="/wiki/Institute_of_Space_and_Astronautical_Science" title="Institute of Space and Astronautical Science">Institute of Space and Astronautical Science</a> was appointed to be the leader of the mission.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">[2]</a></sup>
</p><p>Despite its designer's intention of a momentary contact, <i>Hayabusa</i> did land and sit on the asteroid surface for about 30 minutes (see <a href="#In_proximity_of_Itokawa">timeline</a> below).
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Mission_profile">Mission profile</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hayabusa&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Mission profile">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<table class="box-More_citations_needed plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content ambox-Refimprove" role="presentation"><tbody><tr><td class="mbox-image"><div style="width:52px"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Question_book-new.svg"><img alt="" data-file-height="399" data-file-width="512" decoding="async" height="39" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/50px-Question_book-new.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/75px-Question_book-new.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/100px-Question_book-new.svg.png 2x" width="50"/></a></div></td><td class="mbox-text"><div class="mbox-text-span">This section <b>needs additional citations for <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability">verification</a></b>.<span class="hide-when-compact"> Please help <a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Hayabusa&amp;action=edit">improve this article</a> by <a href="/wiki/Help:Introduction_to_referencing_with_Wiki_Markup/1" title="Help:Introduction to referencing with Wiki Markup/1">adding citations to reliable sources</a>. Unsourced material may be challenged and removed.<br/><small><span class="plainlinks"><i>Find sources:</i> <a class="external text" href="//www.google.com/search?as_eq=wikipedia&amp;q=%22Hayabusa%22" rel="nofollow">"Hayabusa"</a> – <a class="external text" href="//www.google.com/search?tbm=nws&amp;q=%22Hayabusa%22+-wikipedia" rel="nofollow">news</a> <b>·</b> <a class="external text" href="//www.google.com/search?&amp;q=%22Hayabusa%22+site:news.google.com/newspapers&amp;source=newspapers" rel="nofollow">newspapers</a> <b>·</b> <a class="external text" href="//www.google.com/search?tbs=bks:1&amp;q=%22Hayabusa%22+-wikipedia" rel="nofollow">books</a> <b>·</b> <a class="external text" href="//scholar.google.com/scholar?q=%22Hayabusa%22" rel="nofollow">scholar</a> <b>·</b> <a class="external text" href="https://www.jstor.org/action/doBasicSearch?Query=%22Hayabusa%22&amp;acc=on&amp;wc=on" rel="nofollow">JSTOR</a></span></small></span> <small class="date-container"><i>(<span class="date">June 2017</span>)</i></small><small class="hide-when-compact"><i> (<a href="/wiki/Help:Maintenance_template_removal" title="Help:Maintenance template removal">Learn how and when to remove this template message</a>)</i></small></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>The <i>Hayabusa</i> spacecraft was launched on 9 May 2003 at 04:29:25 UTC on an <a href="/wiki/M-V" title="M-V">M-V</a> rocket from the <a href="/wiki/Uchinoura_Space_Center" title="Uchinoura Space Center">Uchinoura Space Center</a> (still called <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kagoshima_Space_Center" title="Kagoshima Space Center">Kagoshima Space Center</a> at that time). Following launch, the spacecraft's name was changed from the original MUSES-C to <i>Hayabusa</i>, the Japanese word for <a href="/wiki/Falcon" title="Falcon">falcon</a>. The spacecraft's xenon <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ion_engine" title="Ion engine">ion engines</a> (four separate units), operating near-continuously for two years, slowly moved <i>Hayabusa</i> toward a September 2005 rendezvous with Itokawa. As it arrived, the spacecraft did not go into orbit around the asteroid, but remained in a station-keeping <a href="/wiki/Heliocentric_orbit" title="Heliocentric orbit">heliocentric orbit</a> close by.
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Hayabusa_IAC_2010.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1944" data-file-width="2592" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3a/Hayabusa_IAC_2010.jpg/220px-Hayabusa_IAC_2010.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3a/Hayabusa_IAC_2010.jpg/330px-Hayabusa_IAC_2010.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3a/Hayabusa_IAC_2010.jpg/440px-Hayabusa_IAC_2010.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Hayabusa_IAC_2010.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The half-scale model of <i>Hayabusa</i> at the IAC in 2010</div></div></div>
<p><i>Hayabusa</i> surveyed the asteroid surface from a distance of about 20 km (13.7 mi), (the "gate position"). After this the spacecraft moved closer to the surface (the "home position"), and then approached the asteroid for a series of soft landings and for the collection of samples at a safe site. <a href="/wiki/Autonomous_robot" title="Autonomous robot">Autonomous</a> optical navigation was employed extensively during this period because the long communication delay prohibits Earth-based real-time commanding. At the second <i>Hayabusa</i> touched down with its deployable collection horn, the spacecraft was programmed to fire tiny projectiles at the surface and then collect the resulting spray. Some tiny specks were collected by the spacecraft for analysis back on Earth.
</p><p>After a few months in proximity to the asteroid, the spacecraft was scheduled to fire its engines to begin its cruise back to Earth. This maneuver was delayed due to problems with <a href="/wiki/Attitude_control" title="Attitude control">attitude control</a> (orientation) and the thrusters of the craft. Once it was on its return trajectory, the re-entry capsule was released from the main spacecraft three hours before reentry, and the capsule coasted on a ballistic trajectory, re-entering the Earth's atmosphere at 13:51, 13 June 2010 UTC.  It is estimated that the capsule experienced peak deceleration of about 25 G and heating rates approximately 30 times those experienced by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Apollo_spacecraft" title="Apollo spacecraft">Apollo spacecraft</a>.  It landed via parachute near <a href="/wiki/Woomera,_South_Australia" title="Woomera, South Australia">Woomera</a>, Australia.
</p><p>In relation to the mission profile, JAXA defined the following success criteria and corresponding scores for major milestones in the mission prior to the launch of the <i>Hayabusa</i> spacecraft.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup> As it shows, the <i>Hayabusa</i> spacecraft is a platform for testing new technology and the primary objective of the <i>Hayabusa</i> project is the world's first implementation of microwave discharge <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ion_engine" title="Ion engine">ion engines</a>.  Hence 'operation of ion engines for more than 1000 hours' is an achievement that gives a full score of 100 points, and the rest of the milestones are a series of world's first-time experiments built on it.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Replica_of_Hayabusa_capsule_at_JAXA_i.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1877" data-file-width="2497" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3e/Replica_of_Hayabusa_capsule_at_JAXA_i.jpg/220px-Replica_of_Hayabusa_capsule_at_JAXA_i.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3e/Replica_of_Hayabusa_capsule_at_JAXA_i.jpg/330px-Replica_of_Hayabusa_capsule_at_JAXA_i.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3e/Replica_of_Hayabusa_capsule_at_JAXA_i.jpg/440px-Replica_of_Hayabusa_capsule_at_JAXA_i.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Replica_of_Hayabusa_capsule_at_JAXA_i.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The replica of the re-entry capsule exhibited at JAXAi (closed on December 28, 2010)</div></div></div>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="MINERVA_mini-lander">MINERVA mini-lander</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hayabusa&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: MINERVA mini-lander">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/MINERVA_(spacecraft)" title="MINERVA (spacecraft)">MINERVA (spacecraft)</a></div>
<p><i>Hayabusa</i> carried a tiny mini-<a href="/wiki/Lander_(spacecraft)" title="Lander (spacecraft)">lander</a> (weighing only 591 g (20.8 oz), and approximately 10 cm (3.9 in) tall by 12 cm (4.7 in) in diameter) named "<i>MINERVA</i>" (short for <i>MI</i>cro-<i>N</i>ano <i>E</i>xperimental <i>R</i>obot <i>V</i>ehicle for the <i>A</i>steroid). An error during deployment resulted in the craft's failure.
</p><p>This solar-powered vehicle was designed to take advantage of Itokawa's very low gravity by using an internal flywheel assembly to hop across the surface of the asteroid, relaying images from its cameras to <i>Hayabusa</i> whenever the two spacecraft were in sight of one another.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup>
</p><p>MINERVA was deployed on 12 November 2005. The lander release command was sent from Earth, but before the command could arrive, <i>Hayabusa</i>'s altimeter measured its distance from Itokawa to be 44 m (144 ft) and thus started an automatic altitude keeping sequence. As a result, when the MINERVA release command arrived, MINERVA was released while the probe was ascending and at a higher altitude than intended, so that it escaped Itokawa's gravitational pull and tumbled into space.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup>
</p><p>Had it been successful, MINERVA would have been the first hopping space rover. The Soviet mission <i><a href="/wiki/Phobos_2" title="Phobos 2">Phobos 2</a></i> also encountered a malfunction while attempting to deploy a hopping rover.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Scientific_and_engineering_importance_of_the_mission">Scientific and engineering importance of the mission</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hayabusa&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Scientific and engineering importance of the mission">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Scientists' current understanding of asteroids depends greatly on meteorite samples, but it is very difficult to match up meteorite samples with the exact asteroids from which they came. <i>Hayabusa</i> will help solve this problem by bringing back pristine samples from a specific, well-characterized asteroid. <i>Hayabusa</i> bridged the gap between ground observation data of asteroids and laboratory analysis of meteorite and <a href="/wiki/Cosmic_dust" title="Cosmic dust">cosmic dust</a> collections.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup> Also comparing the data from the onboard instruments of the <i>Hayabusa</i> with the data from the <a href="/wiki/NEAR_Shoemaker" title="NEAR Shoemaker"><i>NEAR Shoemaker</i> mission</a> will put the knowledge on a wider level.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (July 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>The <i>Hayabusa</i> mission has a very deep engineering importance for JAXA, too. It allowed JAXA to further test its technologies in the fields of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ion_engine" title="Ion engine">ion engines</a>, autonomous and optical navigation, deep space communication, and close movement on objects with low gravity among others. Second, since it was the first-ever preplanned soft contact with the surface of an asteroid (the <i>NEAR Shoemaker</i> landing on 433 Eros was not preplanned) it has enormous influence on further asteroid missions.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (January 2014)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Changes_in_mission_plan">Changes in mission plan</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hayabusa&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Changes in mission plan">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The <i>Hayabusa</i> mission profile was modified several times, both before and after launch.
</p>
<ul><li>The spacecraft was originally intended to launch in July 2002 to the asteroid <a href="/wiki/4660_Nereus" title="4660 Nereus">4660 Nereus</a> (the asteroid <a href="/wiki/(10302)_1989_ML" title="(10302) 1989 ML">(10302) 1989 ML</a> was considered as an alternative target).  However, a July 2000 failure of Japan's <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/M-5_rocket" title="M-5 rocket">M-5 rocket</a> forced a delay in the launch, putting both Nereus and 1989 ML out of reach.  As a result, the target asteroid was changed to <a href="/wiki/25143_Itokawa" title="25143 Itokawa"><span class="nowrap">1998 SF<sub>36</sub></span></a>, which was soon thereafter named for Japanese rocket pioneer <a href="/wiki/Hideo_Itokawa" title="Hideo Itokawa">Hideo Itokawa</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup></li>
<li><i>Hayabusa</i> was to deploy a small rover supplied by <a href="/wiki/NASA" title="NASA">NASA</a> and developed by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/JPL" title="JPL">JPL</a>, called Muses-CN, onto the surface of the asteroid, but the rover was canceled by NASA in November 2000 due to budget constraints.</li>
<li>In 2002, launch was postponed from December 2002 to May 2003 to recheck the <a href="/wiki/O-ring" title="O-ring">O-rings</a> of its <a href="/wiki/Reaction_control_system" title="Reaction control system">reaction control system</a> since one of them had been found to be using a different material than specified.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-isasnews20020925_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-isasnews20020925-9">[9]</a></sup></li>
<li>In 2003, while <i>Hayabusa</i> was en route to Itokawa, the largest <a href="/wiki/Solar_flare" title="Solar flare">solar flare</a> recorded in history<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup> damaged the <a href="/wiki/Solar_cell" title="Solar cell">solar cells</a> aboard the spacecraft.  This reduction in electrical power reduced the efficiency of the ion engines, thus delaying the arrival at Itokawa from June to September 2005.  Since orbital mechanics dictated that the spacecraft still had to leave the asteroid by November 2005, the amount of the time it was able to spend at Itokawa was greatly reduced and the number of landings on the asteroid was reduced from three to two.</li>
<li>In 2005, two <a href="/wiki/Reaction_wheel" title="Reaction wheel">reaction wheels</a> that govern the attitude movement of <i>Hayabusa</i> failed; the X-axis wheel failed on July 31, and the Y-axis on October 2. After the latter failure, the spacecraft was still able to turn on its X and Y axes with its thrusters. JAXA claimed that since global mapping of Itokawa had been completed, this was not a major problem, but the mission plan was altered. The failed reaction wheels were manufactured by Ithaco Space Systems, Inc, New York, which was later acquired by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Goodrich_Company" title="Goodrich Company">Goodrich Company</a>.</li>
<li>The 4 November 2005, 'rehearsal' landing on Itokawa failed, and was rescheduled.</li>
<li>The original decision to sample two different sites on the asteroid was changed when one of the sites, Woomera Desert, was found to be too rocky for a safe landing.</li>
<li>The 12 November 2005, release of the MINERVA miniprobe ended in failure.</li></ul>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Mission_timeline">Mission timeline</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hayabusa&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Mission timeline">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Up_to_the_launch">Up to the launch</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hayabusa&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Up to the launch">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The asteroid exploration mission by the <a href="/wiki/Institute_of_Space_and_Astronautical_Science" title="Institute of Space and Astronautical Science">Institute of Space and Astronautical Science</a> (ISAS) originated in 1986–1987 when the scientists investigated the feasibility of a sample return mission to <a href="/wiki/1943_Anteros" title="1943 Anteros">Anteros</a> and concluded that the technology was not yet developed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup> Between 1987 and 1994, joint ISAS / NASA group studied several missions: an asteroid rendezvous mission later became <a href="/wiki/NEAR_Shoemaker" title="NEAR Shoemaker">NEAR</a>, and a comet sample return mission later became <a href="/wiki/Stardust_(spacecraft)" title="Stardust (spacecraft)">Stardust</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1995, ISAS selected the asteroid sampling as an engineering demonstration mission, MUSES-C, Nereus as the first choice of target, <a href="/wiki/(10302)_1989_ML" title="(10302) 1989 ML">1989 ML</a> as the secondary choice, and MUSES-C project started in fiscal year 1996. In early development phase, Nereus was considered out of reach and 1989 ML became the primary target.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup> July 2000 failure of <a href="/wiki/M-V" title="M-V">M-V</a> forced a delay in the launch from July 2002 to November/December, putting both Nereus and 1989 ML out of reach.  As a result, the target asteroid was changed to <a href="/wiki/25143_Itokawa" title="25143 Itokawa"><span class="nowrap">1998 SF<sub>36</sub></span></a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup> In 2002, launch was postponed from December 2002 to May 2003 to recheck <a href="/wiki/O-ring" title="O-ring">O-rings</a> of <a href="/wiki/Reaction_control_system" title="Reaction control system">reaction control system</a> since one of it was found using different material than specification.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-isasnews20020925_9-1"><a href="#cite_note-isasnews20020925-9">[9]</a></sup> On May 9, 2003 04:29:25 UTC, MUSES-C was launched by <a href="/wiki/M-V" title="M-V">M-V</a> rocket, and the probe was named "<i>Hayabusa</i>".
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Cruising">Cruising</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hayabusa&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Cruising">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Ion thruster checkout started on 27 May 2003.  Full power operation started on 25 June.
</p><p>Asteroids are named by their discoverer.  ISAS asked <a href="/wiki/Lincoln_Near-Earth_Asteroid_Research" title="Lincoln Near-Earth Asteroid Research">LINEAR</a>, the discoverer of <span class="nowrap">1998 SF<sub>36</sub></span>, to offer the name after <a href="/wiki/Hideo_Itokawa" title="Hideo Itokawa">Hideo Itokawa</a>, and on 6 August, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Minor_Planet_Circular" title="Minor Planet Circular">Minor Planet Circular</a> reported that the target asteroid <span class="nowrap">1998 SF<sub>36</sub></span> was named <i>Itokawa</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup>
</p><p>In October 2003, ISAS and two other national aerospace agencies were merged to form <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Japan_Aerospace_Exploration_Agency" title="Japan Aerospace Exploration Agency">JAXA</a>.
</p><p>On 31 March 2004, ion thruster operation was stopped to prepare for the Earth swing-by.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup> Last maneuver operation before swing-by on 12 May.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup> On 19 May, <i>Hayabusa</i> performed Earth swing-by.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup> On 27 May, ion thruster operation was started again.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup>
</p><p>On February 18, 2005, <i>Hayabusa</i> passed aphelion at 1.7 AU.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup> On 31 July, the X-axis reaction wheel failed. On 14 August, <i>Hayabusa</i>'s first image of Itokawa was released. The picture was taken by the star tracker and shows a point of light, believed to be the asteroid, moving across the starfield.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup> Other images were taken from 22 to 24 August.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup> On 28 August, <i>Hayabusa</i> was switched over from the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ion_engine" title="Ion engine">ion engines</a> to the bi-propellant thrusters for orbital maneuvering. From 4 September, <i>Hayabusa</i>'s cameras were able to confirm Itokawa's elongated shape.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup> From 11 September, individual hills were discerned on the asteroid.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup> On 12 September, <i>Hayabusa</i> was 20 km (12 mi) from Itokawa and JAXA scientists announced that <i>Hayabusa</i> had officially "arrived".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="In_proximity_of_Itokawa">In proximity of Itokawa</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hayabusa&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: In proximity of Itokawa">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>On 15 September 2005, a 'colour' image of the asteroid was released (which is, however, grey in colouring).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup> On 4 October, JAXA announced that the spacecraft had successfully moved to its 'Home Position' 7 km from Itokawa. Closeup pictures were released. It was also announced that the spacecraft's second reaction wheel, governing the Y-axis, had failed, and that the craft was now being pointed by its rotation thrusters.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup> On November 3, <i>Hayabusa</i> took station 3.0 km from Itokawa. It then began its descent, planned to include delivery of a target marker, and release of the Minerva minilander. The descent went well initially, and navigation images with wide-angle cameras were obtained. However, at 01:50 <a href="/wiki/Coordinated_Universal_Time" title="Coordinated Universal Time">UTC</a> (<span class="nowrap">10:50 am</span> <a href="/wiki/Japan_Standard_Time" title="Japan Standard Time">JST</a>) on 4 November, it was announced that due to a detection of an anomalous signal at the Go/NoGo decision, the descent, including release of Minerva and the target marker had been canceled.  The project manager, Junichiro Kawaguchi, explained that the optical navigation system was not tracking the asteroid very well, probably caused by the complex shape of Itokawa.  A few days delay was required to evaluate the situation and reschedule.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup>
</p><p>On 7 November, <i>Hayabusa</i> was 7.5 km from Itokawa. On November 9, <i>Hayabusa</i> performed a descent to <span class="nowrap">70 m</span> to test the landing navigation and the laser altimeter. After that, <i>Hayabusa</i> backed off to a higher position, then descended again to <span class="nowrap">500 m</span> and released one of the target markers into space to test the craft's ability to track it (this was confirmed). From analysis of the closeup images, the Woomera Desert site (Point B) was found to be too rocky to be suitable for landing. The Muses Sea site (Point A) was selected as the landing site, for both first and, if possible, second landings.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup>
</p><p>On 12 November, <i>Hayabusa</i> closed in to <span class="nowrap">55 m</span> from the asteroid's surface.  MINERVA was released but due to an error failed to reach the surface. On 19 November, <i>Hayabusa</i> landed on the asteroid. There was considerable confusion during and after the maneuver about precisely what had happened, because the high-gain antenna of the probe could not be used during final phase of touch-down, as well as the blackout during handover of ground station antenna from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Deep_Space_Network" title="Deep Space Network">DSN</a> to Usuda station.  It was initially reported that <i>Hayabusa</i> had stopped at approximately 10 meters from the surface, hovering for 30 minutes for unknown reasons. Ground control sent a command to abort and ascend, and by the time the communication was regained, the probe had moved 100 km away from the asteroid.  The probe had entered into a <a href="/wiki/Safe_mode_(spacecraft)" title="Safe mode (spacecraft)">safe mode</a>, slowly spinning to stabilize <a href="/wiki/Attitude_control" title="Attitude control">attitude control</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup> However, after regaining control and communication with the probe, the data from the landing attempt were downloaded and analyzed, and on 23 November, JAXA announced that the probe had indeed landed on the asteroid's surface.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup> Unfortunately, the sampling sequence was not triggered since a sensor detected an obstacle during descent; the probe tried to abort the landing, but since its orientation was not appropriate for ascent, it chose instead a safe descent mode. This mode did not permit a sample to be taken, but there is a high probability that some dust may have whirled up into the sampling horn when it touched the asteroid, so the sample canister currently attached to the sampling horn was sealed.  On November 25, a second touchdown attempt was performed. It was initially thought that this time, the sampling device was activated;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup> however, later analysis decided that this was probably another failure and that no pellets were fired.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup> Due to a leak in the thruster system, the probe was put in a "safe hold mode".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup>
</p><p>On 30 November, JAXA announced that control and communication with <i>Hayabusa</i> had been restored, but a problem remained with the craft's <a href="/wiki/Reaction_control_system" title="Reaction control system">reaction control system</a>, perhaps involving a frozen pipe. Mission control was working to resolve the problem before the craft's upcoming <a href="/wiki/Launch_window" title="Launch window">launch window</a> for return to Earth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup> On December 6, <i>Hayabusa</i> was 550 km from Itokawa.  JAXA held a press conference about the situation so far.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup> On 27 November, the probe experienced a power outage when trying to reorient the spacecraft, probably due to a fuel leakage. On 2 December, an attitude (orientation) correction was attempted, but the thruster did not generate enough force. On 3 December, the probe's Z-axis was found to be 20 to 30 degrees from the Sun direction and increasing.   On 4 December, as an emergency measure, xenon propellant from the ion engines was blown to correct the spin, and it was confirmed successful.  On 5 December, <a href="/wiki/Attitude_control" title="Attitude control">attitude control</a>  was corrected enough to regain communication through the medium gain antenna.  Telemetry was obtained and analyzed. As the result of telemetry analysis, it was found that there was a strong possibility that the sampler projectile had not penetrated when it landed on 25 November.  Due to the power outage, the <a href="/wiki/Telemetry" title="Telemetry">telemetry</a> log data was faulty. On 8 December, a sudden orientation change was observed, and communication with <i>Hayabusa</i> was lost.  It was thought likely that the turbulence was caused by evaporation of 8 or 10<a href="/wiki/Cubic_metre#Multiples_and_submultiples" title="Cubic metre">cc</a> of leaked fuel.  This forced a wait of a month or two for <i>Hayabusa</i> to stabilize by conversion of <a href="/wiki/Precession" title="Precession">precession</a> to pure rotation, after which the rotation axis needed to be directed toward the Sun and Earth within a specific angular range.  The probability of achieving this was estimated at 60% by December 2006, 70% by spring 2007.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Recovery_and_return_to_Earth">Recovery and return to Earth</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hayabusa&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Recovery and return to Earth">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Animation_of_Hayabusa_trajectory_returning_from_Itokawa_to_Earth.gif"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="420" data-file-width="560" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/34/Animation_of_Hayabusa_trajectory_returning_from_Itokawa_to_Earth.gif/220px-Animation_of_Hayabusa_trajectory_returning_from_Itokawa_to_Earth.gif" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/34/Animation_of_Hayabusa_trajectory_returning_from_Itokawa_to_Earth.gif/330px-Animation_of_Hayabusa_trajectory_returning_from_Itokawa_to_Earth.gif 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/34/Animation_of_Hayabusa_trajectory_returning_from_Itokawa_to_Earth.gif/440px-Animation_of_Hayabusa_trajectory_returning_from_Itokawa_to_Earth.gif 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Animation_of_Hayabusa_trajectory_returning_from_Itokawa_to_Earth.gif" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Animation of Hayabusa<span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">'s</span> trajectory returning from <a href="/wiki/25143_Itokawa" title="25143 Itokawa">Itokawa</a> to <a href="/wiki/Earth" title="Earth">Earth</a>.<br/><span style="margin:0; font-size:90%; white-space:nowrap;"><span class="legend-text" style="border:none; padding:1px .6em; background-color:magenta; color:black; font-size:95%; line-height:1.25; text-align:center;"> </span>  Hayabusa</span><span style="margin:0; font-size:90%; white-space:nowrap;"><span class="legend-text" style="border:none; padding:1px .6em; background-color:Cyan; color:black; font-size:95%; line-height:1.25; text-align:center;"> </span>  <a href="/wiki/25143_Itokawa" title="25143 Itokawa">Itokawa</a></span> <b>·</b> <span style="margin:0; font-size:90%; white-space:nowrap;"><span class="legend-text" style="border:none; padding:1px .6em; background-color: RoyalBlue ; color:black; font-size:95%; line-height:1.25; text-align:center;"> </span> <a href="/wiki/Earth" title="Earth">Earth</a></span> <b>·</b> <span style="margin:0; font-size:90%; white-space:nowrap;"><span class="legend-text" style="border:none; padding:1px .6em; background-color: Yellow; color:black; font-size:95%; line-height:1.25; text-align:center;"> </span> <a href="/wiki/Sun" title="Sun">Sun</a></span></div></div></div>
<p>On 7 March 2006, JAXA announced<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup> that communication with <i>Hayabusa</i> had been recovered in the following stages: On 23 January, the beacon signal from the probe was detected. On 26 January, the probe responded to commands from ground control by changing the beacon signal. On 6 February, an ejection of xenon propellant was commanded for attitude control to improve communication. The spin axis change rate was about two degrees per day. On 25 February, telemetry data was obtained through the low-gain antenna. On 4 March, telemetry data was obtained through the medium-gain antenna. On 6 March, <i>Hayabusa'</i>s position was established at about 13,000 km ahead of Itokawa in its orbit with a relative speed of <span class="nowrap">3 m</span> per second.
</p><p>On 1 June, <i>Hayabusa</i> project manager Junichiro Kawaguchi reported<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup> that they confirmed two out of four ion engines were working normally, which would have been sufficient for the return journey. On 30 January 2007, Jaxa reported that 7 out of 11 batteries were working and the return capsule was sealed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup> On 25 April, JAXA reported that <i>Hayabusa</i> started the return journey.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup> On 29 August, it was announced that Ion Engine C onboard Hayabusa, in addition to B and D, had been successfully re-ignited.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup> On 29 October, JAXA reported that the first phase of trajectory maneuver operation had finished and the spacecraft was now put in a spin-stabilized state.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup> On 4 February 2009, JAXA reported success in reignition of the ion engines and starting the second phase of the trajectory correction maneuver to return to Earth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup> On 4 November 2009, the ion engine D automatically stopped working due to an anomaly from degradation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup>
</p><p>On 19 November 2009, JAXA announced that they managed to combine the ion generator of ion engine B and the neutralizer of ion engine A.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup> It was suboptimal but expected to be sufficient to generate the necessary delta-v. Out of <span class="nowrap">2,200 m/s</span> <a href="/wiki/Delta-v" title="Delta-v">delta-v</a> necessary to return to the Earth, about <span class="nowrap">2,000 m/s</span> had been performed already, and about <span class="nowrap">200 m/s</span> were still necessary.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup> On 5 March 2010, <i>Hayabusa</i> was on a trajectory that would have passed within the lunar orbit. Ion engine operation was suspended to measure the precise trajectory in preparation to perform Trajectory Correction Maneuver 1 to the Earth-rim trajectory.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup> On 27 March, 06:17 UTC, <i>Hayabusa</i> was on a trajectory which would pass 20,000 km from Earth center, completing the orbit transfer operation from Itokawa to Earth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup> By 6 April, the first stage of Trajectory Correction Maneuver (TCM-0) which led to a rough Earth rim trajectory was completed. It was planned to be 60 days until reentry.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-jaxa20100421_64-0"><a href="#cite_note-jaxa20100421-64">[64]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20100331_sac_hayabusa_67-0"><a href="#cite_note-20100331_sac_hayabusa-67">[67]</a></sup> By 4 May, the probe completed its TCM-1 maneuver to align precisely to the Earth rim trajectory.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup> On 22 May, TCM-2 started, continued for about 92.5 hours, and finished on 26 May.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup> It was followed by TCM-3 from 3 through 5 June to change the trajectory from the Earth rim to <a href="/wiki/Woomera,_South_Australia" title="Woomera, South Australia">Woomera, South Australia</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup> TCM-4 was performed on June 9 for about 2.5 hours for a precision descent to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Woomera_Prohibited_Area" title="Woomera Prohibited Area">Woomera Prohibited Area</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup>
</p><p>The reentry capsule was released at 10:51 UTC on 13 June.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Reentry_and_capsule_retrieval">Reentry and capsule retrieval</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hayabusa&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Reentry and capsule retrieval">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Hayabusa_reentry_from_Ames_Research_2010-06-13_25seconds.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="187" data-file-width="618" decoding="async" height="67" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/18/Hayabusa_reentry_from_Ames_Research_2010-06-13_25seconds.png/220px-Hayabusa_reentry_from_Ames_Research_2010-06-13_25seconds.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/18/Hayabusa_reentry_from_Ames_Research_2010-06-13_25seconds.png/330px-Hayabusa_reentry_from_Ames_Research_2010-06-13_25seconds.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/18/Hayabusa_reentry_from_Ames_Research_2010-06-13_25seconds.png/440px-Hayabusa_reentry_from_Ames_Research_2010-06-13_25seconds.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Hayabusa_reentry_from_Ames_Research_2010-06-13_25seconds.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The glowing return capsule is seen forward of and below the parent <i>Hayabusa</i> probe bus as the latter breaks up.</div></div></div>
<p>The reentry capsule and the spacecraft reentered Earth's atmosphere on 13 June 2010 at 13:51 UTC (23:21 local).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Heat_shield" title="Heat shield">heat-shielded</a> capsule made a parachute landing in the South <a href="/wiki/Outback" title="Outback">Australian outback</a> while the spacecraft broke up and incinerated in a large fireball.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup>
</p><p>An international team of scientists observed the 12.2 km/s entry of the capsule from 11.9 km (39,000 ft) on board NASA's DC-8 airborne laboratory, using a wide array of imaging and spectrographic cameras to measure the physical conditions during <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Atmospheric_reentry" title="Atmospheric reentry">atmospheric reentry</a> in a mission led by NASA's <a href="/wiki/Ames_Research_Center" title="Ames Research Center">Ames Research Center</a>, with <a href="/wiki/Peter_Jenniskens" title="Peter Jenniskens">Peter Jenniskens</a> of the <a href="/wiki/SETI_Institute" title="SETI Institute">SETI Institute</a> as the project scientist.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup>
</p><p>Since the reaction control system no longer functioned, the 510 kilograms (1,120 lb) space probe re-entered the Earth's atmosphere similar to the approach of an <a href="/wiki/Asteroid" title="Asteroid">asteroid</a> along with the sample re-entry capsule, and, as mission scientists expected, the majority of the spacecraft disintegrated upon entry.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Hayabusa_Re-Entery.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2020" data-file-width="3212" decoding="async" height="138" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2b/Hayabusa_Re-Entery.jpg/220px-Hayabusa_Re-Entery.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2b/Hayabusa_Re-Entery.jpg/330px-Hayabusa_Re-Entery.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2b/Hayabusa_Re-Entery.jpg/440px-Hayabusa_Re-Entery.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Hayabusa_Re-Entery.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The re-entry seen from the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Woomera_Test_Range" title="Woomera Test Range">Woomera Test Range</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>The return capsule was predicted to land in a <span class="nowrap">20 km</span> by <span class="nowrap">200 km</span> area in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Woomera_Prohibited_Area" title="Woomera Prohibited Area">Woomera Prohibited Area</a>, <a href="/wiki/South_Australia" title="South Australia">South Australia</a>. Four ground teams surrounded this area and located the re-entry capsule by optical observation and a radio beacon. Then a team on board a helicopter was dispatched. They located the capsule and recorded its position with GPS. The capsule was successfully retrieved at 07:08 UTC (16:38 local) of 14 June 2010.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[78]</a></sup> The two parts of the heat shield, which were jettisoned during the descent, were also found.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bbcnews10307048_79-0"><a href="#cite_note-bbcnews10307048-79">[79]</a></sup>
</p>

codecs=&amp;quot;vp8, vorbis&amp;quot;" data-title="WebM (360P)" data-shorttitle="WebM 360P" data-transcodekey="360p.webm" data-width="640" data-height="274" data-bandwidth="508048" data-framerate="29.97002997003"/&gt;&lt;/video&gt;&lt;/div&gt;'><img alt="File:Hayabusa reentry from Ames Research 2010-06-13.ogv" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0a/Hayabusa_reentry_from_Ames_Research_2010-06-13.ogv/220px--Hayabusa_reentry_from_Ames_Research_2010-06-13.ogv.jpg" style="width:220px;height:94px"/><a href="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/0/0a/Hayabusa_reentry_from_Ames_Research_2010-06-13.ogv" target="new" title="Play media"><span class="play-btn-large"><span class="mw-tmh-playtext">Play media</span></span></a></div> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Hayabusa_reentry_from_Ames_Research_2010-06-13.ogv" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i>Hayabusa</i> re-entry filmed by a camera onboard NASA's DC-8 Airborne Laboratory. The glowing return capsule is seen forward of and below the main <i>Hayabusa</i> probe bus as the latter breaks up. The <a href="/wiki/Heat_shield" title="Heat shield">heat-shielded</a> capsule continues leaving a wake after the main bus fragments have faded. (<a href="/wiki/File:Hayabusa_atmospheric_reentry_(close-up).ogv" title="File:Hayabusa atmospheric reentry (close-up).ogv">Close-up video</a>)</div></div></div>
<p>After confirming that the explosive devices used for parachute deployment were safe the capsule was packed inside a double layer of plastic bags filled with pure nitrogen gas to reduce the risk of contamination.  The soil at the landing site was also sampled for reference in case of contamination.  Then the capsule was put inside a cargo container which had air suspension to keep the capsule below 1.5 G shock during transportation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup>  The capsule and its heat shield parts were transported to Japan by a chartered plane and arrived at the curation facility at the JAXA/ISAS Sagamihara campus on June 18.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup>
</p><p>A <a href="/wiki/Tokyo_Metropolitan_Government" title="Tokyo Metropolitan Government">Tokyo Metropolitan Government</a> adviser and former lieutenant general, Toshiyuki Shikata, claimed that part of the rationale for the reentry and landing part of the mission was to demonstrate "that Japan's ballistic missile capability is credible."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[82]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Scientific_study_of_samples">Scientific study of samples</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hayabusa&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Scientific study of samples">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Before the capsule was extracted from the protecting plastic bag, it was inspected using <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/X-ray_computed_tomography" title="X-ray computed tomography">X-ray CT</a> to determine its condition. Then the sample canister was extracted from the reentry capsule. The surface of the canister was cleaned using pure nitrogen gas and carbon dioxide; it was then placed in the canister opening device. The internal pressure of the canister was determined by a slight deformation of the canister as the pressure of the environment nitrogen gas in the clean chamber was varied. The nitrogen gas pressure was then adjusted to match the internal canister pressure to prevent the escape of any gas from the sample upon the opening of the canister.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[83]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Confirmation_of_asteroid_particles">Confirmation of asteroid particles</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hayabusa&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Confirmation of asteroid particles">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>On 16 November 2010, JAXA confirmed that most of the particles found in one of two compartments inside the <i>Hayabusa</i> sample return capsule came from Itokawa.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[84]</a></sup> Analysis with a scanning electron microscope identified about 1,500 grains as rocky particles, according to the JAXA press release.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[85]</a></sup> After further studying the analysis results and comparison of mineral compositions, most of them were judged to be of extraterrestrial origin, and definitely from the asteroid Itokawa.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[86]</a></sup>
</p><p>According to Japanese scientists, the composition of <i>Hayabusa</i>'s samples was more similar to <a href="/wiki/Meteorite" title="Meteorite">meteorites</a> than known rocks from Earth. Their size is mostly less than 10 micrometers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[87]</a></sup> The material matches chemical maps of Itokawa from <i>Hayabusa'</i>s remote sensing instruments. The researchers found concentrations of <a href="/wiki/Olivine" title="Olivine">olivine</a> and <a href="/wiki/Pyroxene" title="Pyroxene">pyroxene</a> in the <i>Hayabusa</i> samples.
</p><p>Further study of the samples had to wait until 2011 because researchers were still developing special handling procedures to avoid contaminating the particles during the next phase of research.
</p><p>In 2013 JAXA announced that 1500 extraterrestrial grains had been recovered, comprising the minerals <a href="/wiki/Olivine" title="Olivine">olivine</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pyroxene" title="Pyroxene">pyroxene</a>, <a href="/wiki/Plagioclase" title="Plagioclase">plagioclase</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Iron_sulphide" title="Iron sulphide">iron sulphide</a>. The grains were about 10 micrometers in size.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[88]</a></sup> JAXA performed detailed analyses of the samples by splitting particles and examining their crystal structure at <a href="/wiki/SPring-8" title="SPring-8">SPring-8</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[89]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Results">Results</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hayabusa&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Results">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The August 26, 2011, issue of <a href="/wiki/Science_(journal)" title="Science (journal)"><i>Science</i></a> devoted six articles to findings based on dust collected by <i>Hayabusa</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nyt25aug_90-0"><a href="#cite_note-nyt25aug-90">[90]</a></sup> Scientists' analysis of the dust from Itokawa suggested that it had probably originally been part of a larger asteroid. Dust collected from the asteroid surface was believed to have been exposed there for about eight million years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nyt25aug_90-1"><a href="#cite_note-nyt25aug-90">[90]</a></sup>
</p><p>Dust from Itokawa was found to be "identical to material that makes up meteorites."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nyt25aug_90-2"><a href="#cite_note-nyt25aug-90">[90]</a></sup> Itokawa is an <a href="/wiki/S-type_asteroid" title="S-type asteroid">S-type asteroid</a> whose composition matches that of an <a href="/wiki/LL_chondrite" title="LL chondrite">LL chondrite</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-latimes26aug_91-0"><a href="#cite_note-latimes26aug-91">[91]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="In_popular_culture">In popular culture</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hayabusa&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: In popular culture">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>In Japan, rival film companies announced the production of three different feature length theatrical films based on the story of <i>Hayabusa</i>, one of which, <i><a href="/wiki/Hayabusa:_Harukanaru_Kikan" title="Hayabusa: Harukanaru Kikan">Hayabusa: Harukanaru Kikan</a></i>, starred <a href="/wiki/Ken_Watanabe" title="Ken Watanabe">Ken Watanabe</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92"><a href="#cite_note-92">[92]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[93]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Lego" title="Lego">Lego</a> construction toy company released a model of <i>Hayabusa</i> through their Cuusoo website.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94">[94]</a></sup>
</p><p>Many references to <i>Hayabusa</i> appear in the Japanese series <i><a href="/wiki/Kamen_Rider_Fourze" title="Kamen Rider Fourze">Kamen Rider Fourze</a></i>, a space-themed <a href="/wiki/Tokusatsu" title="Tokusatsu">tokusatsu</a> series.
</p><p>A music video titled "Hayabusa", named after the unmanned spacecraft was made using the <a href="/wiki/Vocaloid" title="Vocaloid">Vocaloid</a> <a href="/wiki/Hatsune_Miku" title="Hatsune Miku">Hatsune Miku</a>. The music and lyrics were composed by SHO.
</p><p>Following the sample return in 2010, Hayabusa-tan (はやぶさたん), an anthropomorphised figure in anime style, was released by the company Aoshima.
</p><p><i>Hayabusa</i> also appears as a playable character in <a href="/wiki/Mobile_Legends:_Bang_Bang" title="Mobile Legends: Bang Bang">Mobile Legends: Bang Bang</a>.
</p>
